    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="8a35906b0c044fb6b8ff787a121f9eec">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '48f1117a-ec03-4e00-9dbe-963f21ff6385'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '03BB7DE67309AF2F9EE0300E3CEAE8FF';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')

                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d53949195" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p><b>The Bs needs</b>: Boron-graphdiyne (BGDY) is prepared through a bottom-up chemical strategy. BGDY has a unique structure with molecular pores decorated with boron atoms. The pure sp-hybridized-carbon skeleton and boron heteroatoms can stabilize the metal atoms intercalated in the BGDY framework. As a result of this unique structure, BGDY shows remarkable electrochemical performance in sodium-ion half cells.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/198bc321-7692-47bc-b4d7-d2e442ced5fd/anie201800453-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/198bc321-7692-47bc-b4d7-d2e442ced5fd/anie201800453-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a0f7e537-684a-4f12-ad49-f3de85565312/anie201800453-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/198bc321-7692-47bc-b4d7-d2e442ced5fd/anie201800453-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d53949198" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Boron-graphdiyne (BGDY), which has a unique π-conjugated structure comprising an sp-hybridized carbon skeleton and evenlydistributed boron heteroatoms in a well-organized 2D molecular plane, is prepared through a bottom-up synthetic strategy. Excellent conductivity, a relatively low band gap and a packing mode of the planar BGDY are observed. Notably, the unusual bonding environment of the all sp-carbon framework and the electron-deficient boron centers generates affinity to metal atoms, and thus provides extra binding sites. Furthermore, the expanded molecule pores of the BGDY molecular plane can also facilitate the transfer of metal ions in the perpendicular direction. The practical effect of the all sp-carbon structure and boron heteroatoms on the properties of BGDY are demonstrated in its performance as the anode in sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie201800453-sec-0001">
         
         <p>Carbon allotropes are capable of forming different architectures with a variety of hybridized forms and molecular shapes, including the sp<sup>3</sup>-hybridized crystal-like diamonds,<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0001_R_d53949187e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a></sup></span> sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized porous covalent organic frameworks (COFs),<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0002_R_d53949187e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Science">2</a></sup></span> sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized spherically shaped fullerenes,<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0003_R_d53949187e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">3</a></sup></span> sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized latticed graphene,<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0004_R_d53949187e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nature">4</a></sup></span> and sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized aromatic nanobelts.<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0005_R_d53949187e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">5</a></sup></span> Compare to the above well-known carbon materials, novel carbon allotropes comprising quantitative content of sp-hybridized carbon have drawn more and more attentions for their unusual predicted and experimental properties including unique electronic structures,<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0006_R_d53949187e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Acc. Chem. Res.">6</a></sup></span> excellent transport properties,<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0007_R_d53949187e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Phys. Rev. B">7</a></sup></span> and electro and chemical affinity.<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0008_R_d53949187e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Catal.">8</a></sup></span> Profiting from the above unique characteristics, sp-carbon rich materials have shown application prospects in many fields, especially in energy transfer and storage devices.<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0009_R_d53949187e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Acta Phys. Chim. Sin.">9</a></sup></span> Moreover, the property and performance of the as-prepared materials are unquestionably highly related to the bonding environment and quantity of sp-hybridized carbon atoms, which can efficiently affect the distribution of the electron cloud in the molecular system, and thus tune its related physical and chemical properties. To optimize the bonding situation, an efficient way is to include a functional group with different electronegativity to the sp-carbon contained system. For example, the successful preparation the one-dimensional sp-hybridized carbon allotropes, such as the linear carbyne, are obstructed by its low stability in the ambient atmosphere, rigorous synthetic conditions and tedious transfer procedure from reaction substrate to the practical device.<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0010_R_d53949187e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Chem.">10</a></sup></span> To overcome these limitations, some metalated moieties<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0011_R_d53949187e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">11</a></sup></span> or end-capping functional groups<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0012_R_d53949187e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Curr. Sci.">12</a></sup></span> have been added to the carbyne. Beyond that, it is also a feasible way to expand the dimension of the linear carbyne and prepare two-dimensional sp-hybridized carbon containing frameworks,<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0013_R_d53949187e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Acc. Chem. Res.">13</a></sup></span> which can be further annealed or doped with other heteroatoms, such as nitrogen,<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0014_R_d53949187e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">14</a></sup></span> halogens,<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0015_R_d53949187e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">15</a></sup></span> and hydrogen,<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0016_R_d53949187e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a></sup></span> to decorate the pores in the molecular plane, and thus provide a broader platform to investigate the intrinsic physicochemical characteristics of the sp-carbon-containing system.<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0017_R_d53949187e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nature Rev. Mater.">17</a></sup></span> Notably, the element boron, which is the neighbor of carbon in the periodic table of elements, can construct a variety of boride materials with unique characters based on the unusual binding situation of boron and connected atoms. It will be interesting to construct a carbon material and combine the advantages of both the sp-hybridized carbon atoms with boron.<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0018_R_d53949187e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">18</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>On the other hand, the quantity of sp-hybridized carbon in alkynyl-group-containing carbon material is another important factor to affect its properties. Recently, graphdiyne, which can be regarded as a sp–sp<sup>2</sup> hybrid carbon allotrope, has been prepared through a synthetic chemical method<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0019_R_d53949187e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Commun.">19</a></sup></span> and drawn much interests for their unique two-dimensional structures and excellent properties.<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0020_R_d53949187e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20">20</a></sup></span> It is clear that the ratio of sp-hybridized carbon to sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized carbon in graphdiyne is 2:1. The inclusion of the quantitative amount of sp carbon in a way of acetylene bonds lead to a significant improvement of electronic structure, which is consistent with the subsequent theoretical analysis.<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0021_R_d53949187e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">21</a></sup></span> Another new carbon allotrope with a higher ratio of sp-hybridized carbon to sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized carbon (4:1) has also showed excellent electrochemical electrode performance<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0022_R_d53949187e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Energy">22</a></sup></span> and enhanced photo-catalytic properties,<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0023_R_d53949187e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a></sup></span> which further confirms the important role of sp-hybridized carbon in the as-prepared two-dimensional carbon allotropes. The development and discovery of new carbon materials and carbon-rich materials with hetero-elements are of scientific significance. It is interesting to prepare a carbon material with an established sp-hybridized carbon component and an even-distribution of a hetero-element, the properties and performance of which should also be of interest.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, boron-graphdiyne (BGDY) is prepared through a bottom-to-up synthesis strategy, which has been verified to be an efficient way to prepare two dimensional carbon materials with precisely controlled and well-organized chemical structure.<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0015_R_d53949187e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">15</a></sup></span> As shown in Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0001">1</a> a, BGDY is a two-dimensional carbon framework composed of evenly distributed boron heteroatoms and sp-hybridized butadiyne linkers, which are quite different from the boron carbides<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0024_R_d53949187e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span> and other boron-doped carbon-rich materials.<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0025_R_d53949187e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">25</a></sup></span> Benefitting from its unique structure, BGDY shows excellent conductivity and electrochemical properties. Meanwhile, theoretical calculations indicate the appropriate synergistic stabilization effect of the boron atoms on sodium atoms, which would provide more storage sites for the intercalated Na atoms. Furthermore, the well-distributing molecule pore size on the BGDY molecular plane would also efficiently reduce the diffusion barrier of Na ions in the perpendicular direction throughout the whole BGDY molecular plane. These notions can be confirmed by the excellent performance of a BGDY-based anode in a practical sodium ion batteries (SIBs).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201800453-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/09f1bb68-8164-4bf2-b3da-858f619989e6/anie201800453-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/09f1bb68-8164-4bf2-b3da-858f619989e6/anie201800453-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dac59589-0c1c-405d-8900-c49f3e976b57/anie201800453-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/09f1bb68-8164-4bf2-b3da-858f619989e6/anie201800453-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201800453-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201800453" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Schematic representation of the experimental setup, the coupling process on copper foil, and the two dimensional structure of BGDY (the inset photograph shows the as-prepared BGDY on the copper foil). The SEM images of the as-prepared BGDY film (b) and the element mapping for boron (c) and carbon (d) of the BGDY film. e) The illustration for the formation process of the aggregate morphology of the BGDY film and the SEM image in high magnification, the green box in the top left corner highlights an aggregated ball of BGDY. f) TEM image of BGDY film exfoliated from the copper substrate. g) HRTEM images of BGDY on ultra-thin carbon film. h) The diameter distribution of the BGDY nanoparticles.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>A BGDY film was prepared through the Glaser–Hay coupling reaction of the monomer, which was prepared through a typical procedure shown in Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0001">1</a> a, Scheme S1 (supporting information) and Figures S1–S3. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0001">1</a> a, the as-prepared BGDY film has a two-dimensional porous carbon-rich framework, in which boron atoms are connected through three butadiyne linkages. The photograph of the BGDY film on copper foil (the inset picture in Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0001">1</a> a) reveals that the dark-brown BGDY film is uniform and continuous. Benefiting from our bottom-up strategy, sp carbon is the only hybridized form of the carbon atoms in BGDY, and boron heteroatoms are evenly distributed in the BGDY framework in an exact and theoretically predicted proportion (14.29 % in atomic number ratio, 13.04 % in weight ratio). To our knowledge, the as-prepared BGDY film is the only reported all sp-hybridized carbon-containing two-dimensional boron carbide material, and may find application in many fields, such as electronic and energy storage devices. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0001">1</a> b, the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image of BGDY film indicates a uniform and compact morphology. The surface of the film is composed of abundant nanoparticles. Element mappings reveal the uniform distribution of the carbon and boron atoms in BGDY (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0001">1</a> c,d). With the presence of acetylenic linkages between repeating patterns of boron heteroatoms, BGDY features a high degree of π-conjunction, and forms 3D nanoparticles (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0001">1</a> e). Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image of the BGDY thin film also shows the aggregation structure formed by the accumulation of some nanoparticles (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0001">1</a> f), which is consistent with the results of SEM results. A high resolution TEM (HRTEM) images revealed some relative multiple lattice fringes (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0001">1</a> g). The size distribution of BGDY nanoparticles is in a range of 24–36 nm (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0001">1</a> h).</p>
         
         <p>As shown in Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0002">2</a> a, there are two carbon species and one boron moiety in the structure of BGDY. The <sup>13</sup>C solid-state NMR spectrum shows two peaks at δ=89.2 and 66.3 ppm corresponding to two types of sp-hybridized carbon atoms, that is, those which are connected to the boron atoms and those only connected to carbon atoms in butadiyne, respectively. Moreover, <sup>11</sup>B solid-state NMR spectroscopy of BGDY shows a single peak at δ=−1.02 ppm, which indicates the boron center atoms is the only existing form of boron in the BGDY framework (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0002">2</a> b). The carbon and boron species are characterized by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) (Figure S4). In detail, the C 1s peaks of BGDY can be mainly deconvoluted into four subpeaks of C−C (sp) at 284.8 eV, C−B(sp) at 285.1 eV, C−O at 287.5 eV, and C=O at 289.1 eV, respectively (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0002">2</a> c). Furthermore, the area ratio of the two sp carbon atoms is close to 1:1, consistent with the structure of BGDY shown in Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0002">2</a> a. The existence of C=O and C−O groups in the fitting peaks of carbon may be due to the oxygenation of the acetylene group in the margin of the two dimensional molecular plane. Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0002">2</a> d shows high-resolution XPS scan for B 1s of the BGDY film, the prominent B 1s peaks at 188.7 eV (1S<sup>1/2</sup>) is assigned to boron heteroatom linked with three butadiyne groups, indicating the homogeneous bonding environment for the boron in the carbon-rich framework. The functional groups in BGDY and monomer were further investigated by Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR) spectrum (Figure S5). The Raman spectra (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0002">2</a> e) of the sample exhibit three main peaks. A peak at 1095 cm<sup>−1</sup> is attributed to the stretching vibration B−C bond, the other two peaks at 2136 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1879 cm<sup>−1</sup> are ascribed to acetylenic bond.<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0026_R_d53949187e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">26</a></sup></span> It should be noted that no peak can be assign to the aromatic sp<sup>2</sup> carbon at the range of 1300–1600 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which confirms that only sp-hybridized carbon is in the BGDY framework. Moreover no significant changes can be observed in the Raman spectra of the BGDY powder exposed to air for sixty days, which can confirms the stability of the BGDY. All the above characterization results verify the formation of the carbon-rich structure BGDY. Notably, the BGDY film shows an excellent conductivity, which is measured to be 1.33×10<sup>−2</sup> S m<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0002">2</a> f). This could be ascribed to the all sp-hybridized form of carbon and the even-distribution of the boron heteroatoms in the BGDY molecular plane.<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0022_R_d53949187e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Energy">22</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201800453-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/50ef0b9c-6d81-493b-a303-90274463b332/anie201800453-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/50ef0b9c-6d81-493b-a303-90274463b332/anie201800453-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8719345c-d795-4ec7-a0d6-a8e56a5592c0/anie201800453-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/50ef0b9c-6d81-493b-a303-90274463b332/anie201800453-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201800453-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201800453" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p><sup>13</sup>C (a) and <sup>11</sup>B (b) solid-state NMR spectrum of BGDY. c),d) XPS spectra of BGDY. e) Raman spectra of BGDY. f) Conductivity measurement of BGDY film on the copper substrate, the inset shows the layered structure of the device comprising copper electrode (yellow), BGDY film (blue), and silver electrode (gray white).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The UV/Vis absorption spectrum indicates that the optical band gap between the highest occupied molecular orbit (HOMO) and the lowest unoccupied molecular orbit (LUMO) of the BGDY is 1.1 eV (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0003">3</a> a), which is a relative low band gap value among the reported sp-hybridized-carbon-contained graphdiyne materials.<span><sup><a href="#anie201800453-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201800453-bib-0024_R_d53949187e636" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span> This value might be due to the quantitative inclusion of electron deficient boron in the BGDY framework. The density functional theory (DFT) is applied to calculate the energy band structure in the Brillouin zone (Figure S6), which indicates that the BGDY monolayer is direct band gap semiconductors at <i>Γ</i> point (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0003">3</a> b). The calculated band-gap energy of BGDY is 1.2 eV, which is consistent well with the measured optical band gap (1.1 eV). The mismatch between the physical and optical band gaps might derive from the exciton effect. Moreover, according to the calculation results (Figure S7), the LUMO of BGDY is mainly determined by the p<sub><i>xy</i></sub> orbitals of C3 and 1C, however, the HOMO derives from the p<sub><i>z</i></sub> orbital of C3 and boron atom. The XRD result of the as-synthesized samples is shown in Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0003">3</a> c. The peaks at 22.092 degree for BGDY correspond to the interlayer spacing of 4.02 Å, which is a comparable interlayer distance to that of graphdiyne (4.05 Å). The XRD diffraction peak of BGDY is broad.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201800453-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4d2914d7-623d-4905-9017-e3f698be4acc/anie201800453-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4d2914d7-623d-4905-9017-e3f698be4acc/anie201800453-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0ce83af5-3070-4e7b-b913-31c61b615d46/anie201800453-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4d2914d7-623d-4905-9017-e3f698be4acc/anie201800453-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201800453-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201800453" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) UV/Vis spectrum of BGDY. b) Calculated electronic band structure of BGDY. Points of high symmetry in the first Brillouin zone are labelled as follows: <i>Γ</i> (0, 0, 0), K (−1/3, 2/3, 0), M (0, 1/2, 0); the inset shows the electron distribution of the HOMO and LUMO energy level. c) XRD pattern of BGDY and calculated diffraction angle, the inset shows the binding energy of optimized configurations. d) The simulated and experimental pore size distribution for BGDY. e) The calculated stacking configuration of bilayer BGDY. L=simulated pore size, shown as blue disk.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To investigate the geometries of bilayer BGDY, four typical stacking orders are considered. The AB2 configuration has the smallest distance with the largest formation energy. But compared with other configurations, the energy of AB2 is little bigger. So we suppose the stacking order should be several orders stacked together, not a single phase configuration (the inset graph in Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0003">3</a> c). Figure S8 shows the nitrogen adsorption/desorption analysis curve of BGDY sample, which indicates the porous packing structure of BGDY containing both mesopores and macropores. Furthermore, the pore size distributions of the BGDY sample are mainly in a range below 0.7 nm, and two maximum values are located at around 0.6 and 0.4 nm (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0003">3</a> d). Moreover, the calculated stacking of bilayer BGDY with four configurations and the corresponding simulated pore sizes are also shown in Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0003">3</a> e. Interestingly, the configuration AB2 which is the most stable based on formation energy has a calculated pore size of 4.3 Å, which is consistent with experimental maximum value. The other configuration AA also shows a relative stable binding energy. The simulated pore size of AA configuration (5.9 Å) is an approximate value compare to another experimental maximum pore size value. These hierarchical pores in BGDY could guarantee the ion diffusion and intercalation, which are crucial for high electrochemical performance.</p>
         
         <p>The performance of BGDY as the anode applied in sodium (Na)-ions battery can help us to investigate the effect of an all sp-hybridized carbon structure with boron heteroatoms. First, we just focused on the Na storage situation of one selected repeating unit in the single BGDY molecular plane. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0004">4</a> a, the stabilities of complexes with the formula (Na)<sub><i>n</i></sub>-C<sub>16</sub>B<sub>2</sub> were examined by their binding energies. When only one Na was added, five possible Na-C<sub>16</sub>B<sub>2</sub> configurations (A1–A5) with the binding energy in a range of −2.24 to −2.71 eV are obtained (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0004">4</a> b). It should be noted that some distortion of butadiyne linkage was observed in these single occupied configurations. This might be due to the strong interaction between the selected repeat unit (C<sub>16</sub>B<sub>2</sub>) and sodium atom with its large size and alkali metallicity. To avoid this irrationality, a strategy of full occupation (see configuration B1–B5 in Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0004">4</a> b) was adopted for our subsequent calculations. It would be closer to the actual storage situation for the sodium atoms symmetrically occupied all the equivalent sites, so that the binding energy of the possible storage sites could be estimated more actually. Based on this, the configuration B1 and B2 showed the most stable binding energy of −1.50 and −1.58 eV, respectively. This results indicated that the storage site on the top of the boron atom (site 1, Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0004">4</a> a) and that on the angle site of the butadiyne linkage and boron in the molecular plane (site 2, Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0004">4</a> a) were suitable for the intercalation of sodium atoms, which might be mainly due to appropriate electronegativity of the boron atoms and butadiyne linkage. Moreover, the storage sites 5 (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0004">4</a> a), which located at the middle of the butadiyne linkage in the molecular plane (see Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0004">4</a> a) also showed a stable binding energy of −1.44 eV. This could further confirm the function of the sp carbon in the storage of sodium. The calculation results indicate that the pure sp-carbon structure and boron atoms are all beneficial to the storage of sodium atoms allowing the suitable interaction between the sodium and molecular pores of BGDY. As shown in Figure S9 and Table S1 the binding energy increases as the number of calculated BGDY molecular layers increases. This can be understood from that the sodium might be simultaneously stabilized by the functional groups of the adjacent two layers, thus a higher binding energy is achieved. Moreover, the diffusion barrier of Na on BGDY is significantly reduced compared to that of graphdiyne (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0004">4</a> c and Figure S10), which is also beneficial to improve the electrochemical performance of BGDY based electrode in SIBs. Based on the above discussion, we try to find the optimized configuration for the maximum Na storage in BGDY based electrode. Considering the binding energy of the possible storage sites and the rigidity of the BGDY framework, a symmetrical configuration in Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0004">4</a> d is selected, which could guarantee both the stability and the maximum Na atoms number intercalated in the system. Finally, we predict that the ratio of carbon and Na in a repetitive unit is 6:5. The calculated capacity value is 1617 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. However, this value is still the lower limit of theoretical capacity, for many pores and defects existing in the multiple layers of BGDY molecular plane can provide more storage sites for Na atoms. The proper interaction between BGDY and Na can be further verified through a comparison experiment, in which Li and K atoms were also put on the suitable storage sites (site 1, 2, and 5, Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0004">4</a> a) in BGDY though the full occupied mode. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0004">4</a> e, much higher binding energy in a range of −2.36 and −2.70 eV for K atom was obtained, and much lower binding energy in a range of −0.59 and −1.08 eV for Li atom was observed, which indicated that strong attraction for K atom and negligible attraction for Li might occur. In comparison, the suitable interaction between the sodium and molecular pores of BGDY would lead to a much smoother process of intercalation and de-intercalation, which also can be verified though experiment results. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0004">4</a> f, the practical electrochemical charge–discharge profiles of the initial three cycles for BGDY films on copper substrate, which contains an inflection point for the capacity while the voltage is at approximately 0.6 V, reflecting the formation process of extensive solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layers (Figure S11). This can be further confirmed by the cyclic voltammogram (CV) curves (Figure S12). BGDY based electrode also shows an excellent rate performance (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0004">4</a> g) and reversible capacity in long cycle (Figure S13), which can maintain at 180 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after even 4000 cycles at a current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie201800453-fig-0004">4</a> h). Moreover, the good conducting properties of BGDY also lead to a more unhindered electron transfer and diffusion of sodium ions in the electrode during cycling (see Figure S14, Table S2). Compared to the GDY, the prominent performance of BGDY electrode might be ascribe to proper electronegativity and size of the decorated molecular pores of BGDY.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201800453-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/568d19bf-22ea-4b94-b35d-b442d357e884/anie201800453-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/568d19bf-22ea-4b94-b35d-b442d357e884/anie201800453-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a3bd8dc6-dd9b-4f26-a2c5-08761ffe9348/anie201800453-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/568d19bf-22ea-4b94-b35d-b442d357e884/anie201800453-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201800453-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201800453" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) The calculated binding energies and corresponding five storage sites (1–5). b) The geometries of optimized single occupied mode Na<sup>+</sup>+C<sub>16</sub>B<sub>2</sub> (A1–A5) and full occupied mode <i>n</i>Na<sup>+</sup>+C<sub>16</sub>B<sub>2</sub> (B1–B5). c) Illustration of the energy potential curves and the corresponding trajectories of Na moving away from the molecular plane of BGDY through the site 2 and 5. d) The possible Na storage sites in BGDY. e) The binding energy between the repeat unit (C<sub>16</sub>B<sub>2</sub>) and Li, Na, K, ions, respectively. f) The charge–discharge profiles of a BGDY electrode at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. g) The rate performance of the electrode for NIBs. h) The cycle performance of the flexible electrode at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In summary, we prepared BGDY film, which had highly conjugated structure and expanded pores on the carbon-rich framework with repeated units of boron heteroatoms connecting through three butadiyne linkages. The unique all sp-hybridized carbon and evenly distributed boron in the BGDY framework endowed BGDY with excellent electronic structure and good conducting properties. Moreover, more stable storage sites for the Na atom and larger transfer tunnels for Na ions in the perpendicular direction of the molecule plane are also revealed. Our results indicate that the inclusion of boron atoms quantitatively and evenly though our bottom-up strategy is an efficient way to improve the electronic and electrochemical performance of boron-doped carbon materials. The new all sp-hybridized carbon-rich flexible BGDY film has shown good potential for application in the field of electronic and energy storage devices.</p>
